## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching

4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Task: Kubernetes Server-Side Apply Configuration Guide

**Repository:** github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0 (mirror of kubernetes/kubernetes) (stripped snapshot)
**Output:** Write your document to `/workspace/documentation.md`

## Objective

Produce a deep guide for applyconfigurations and Server-Side Apply semantics, including conflict handling and workflow patterns.

## Scope

Focus on the applyconfigurations and Server-Side Apply subsystem.
Your document must explain component responsibilities, end-to-end flow, and extension or operational tradeoffs.

## Content Expectations

Address all of the following in your own structure:
- subsystem purpose, boundaries, and upstream/downstream dependencies
- key components and how responsibilities are split
- end-to-end control/request flow with concrete repository evidence
- failure modes, limits, and design tradeoffs
- extension points, customization hooks, and associated risks
- a concise map of the most important files/directories used in your analysis

## Quality Bar

- Cite concrete file paths from the repository.
- Explain behavior and interactions, not just API signatures.
- Include at least one section on operational implications (performance, reliability, or maintainability).
- Do not fabricate classes, functions, or files.

## Anti-Requirements

- Do not output `doc.go`.
- Do not provide shallow bullet dumps without system flow explanation.
- Do not rely on external docs not present in the workspace.

{"content":"200: apiserver uses a **watch cache**. The implementation can be found in\n201: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/cacher/`.\n202: \n203: *   **Initialization:** The cacher first performs a `LIST` to get the current state of all\n204:     objects and a `ResourceVersion` for that point-in-time. It then starts a `WATCH` from\n205:     that version to ensure a consistent stream of events.\n206: *   **Serving from Cache:** Most list and watch requests are served from this in-memory cache, which\n207:     dramatically reduces the load on etcd. Consistent reads are also served from the\n208:     cache. This is achieved by first fetching the revision number of the latest write from\n209:     etcd. The server then ensures the cache is at least that recent—waiting for it to\n210:     refresh if necessary—before serving the request.\n211: *   **Fallback to Storage:** If a client request cannot be served from the\n212:     cache's buffer, the request \"falls through\" to the underlying etcd storage.\n213: *   **Bookmarks:** The cacher uses bookmark events to track the latest `ResourceVersion` for\n214:     unchanged objects. This prevents the cache's `ResourceVersion` from becoming too old,\n215:     which avoids the need for expensive relist operations from etcd when the objects have\n216:     not been modified.\n217: \n218: ## 5. Conflict Resolution\n219: \n220: *   **Optimistic Concurrency via `resourceVersion`:** Clients are expected to perform updates using a\n221:     read-modify-write workflow. The apiserver uses the `resourceVersion` field of every\n222:     object to enforce optimistic concurrency. This `resourceVersion` is not an arbitrary number;\n223:     it maps directly to etcd's globally consistent `mod_revision`. When a client submits an\n224:   
... (truncated)

The documentation has been written to `/workspace/documentation.md`. Here is a summary of what it covers:

**Structure of the document:**

1. **Subsystem Purpose, Boundaries, and Dependencies** — explains SSA's role, the three-package span (`client-go/applyconfigurations`, `apimachinery/util/managedfields`, `apiserver/handlers/patch.go`), and the hard dependency on the external `sigs.k8s.io/structured-merge-diff` library which Kubernetes does not own.

2. **Key Components and Responsibility Split** — details the generated apply configuration types (why all-pointer structs are mandatory, citing the `maxReplicas: 0` footgun from using regular structs), the decorator manager stack, and the PATCH handler's patch-type dispatch.

3. **End-to-End Request Flow** — traces a request from typed client builder call → YAML serialization → PATCH with `application/apply-patch+yaml` → `applyPatcher` → `FieldManager.Apply` → nine-layer decorator chain → SMD library merge → `EncodeObjectManagedFields` → etcd write, with file:line citations throughout.

4. **Extract Workflow** — the `ExtractInto` mechanism with its field-set lookup, `ExtractItems` call, and the OpenAPI-backed `UnstructuredExtractor` with its 1-minute TTL cache.

5. **Conflict Handling** — detection in SMD, the 409 error format with per-field `StatusCause`, force resolution semantics, and the kubectl migration bypass in `lastAppliedManager`.

6. **Subresource Handling** — the `ScaleHandler`'s bidirectional field-path translation between parent and `/scale`.

7. **Failure Modes, Limits, and Design Tradeoffs** — object-too-large behavior difference between apply and non-apply patches, the 10-manager cap (update only, not apply), CRD schemaless atomicity, mandatory nil managedFields in apply requests, version mismatch handlin
... (truncated)

{
  "content": "{\"content\":\"1: /*\\n2: Copyright 2021 The Kubernetes Authors.\\n3: \\n4: Licensed under the Apache License, Version 2.0 (the \\\"License\\\");\\n5: you may not use this file except in compliance with the License.\\n6: You may obtain a copy of the License at\\n7: \\n8:     http://www.apache.org/licenses/LICENSE-2.0\\n9: \\n10: Unless required by applicable law or agreed to in writing, software\\n11: distributed under the License is distributed on an \\\"AS IS\\\" BASIS,\\n12: WITHOUT WARRANTIES OR CONDITIONS OF ANY KIND, either express or implied.\\n13: See the License for the specific language governing permissions and\\n14: limitations under the License.\\n15: */\\n16: \\n17: /*\\n18: Package applyconfigurations provides typesafe go representations of the apply\\n19: configurations that are used to constructs Server-side Apply requests.\\n20: \\n21: # Basics\\n22: \\n23: The Apply functions in the typed client (see the k8s.io/client-go/kubernetes/typed packages) offer\\n24: a direct and typesafe way of calling Server-side Apply. Each Apply function takes an \\\"apply\\n25: configuration\\\" type as an argument, which is a structured representation of an Apply request. For\\n26: example:\\n27: \\n28: \\timport (\\n29: \\t     ...\\n30: \\t     v1ac \\\"k8s.io/client-go/applyconfigurations/autoscaling/v1\\\"\\n31: \\t)\\n32: \\thpaApplyConfig := v1ac.HorizontalPodAutoscaler(autoscalerName, ns).\\n33: \\t     WithSpec(v1ac.HorizontalPodAutoscalerSpec().\\n34: \\t              WithMinReplicas(0)\\n35: \\t     )\\n36: \\treturn hpav1client.Apply(ctx, hpaApplyConfig, metav1.ApplyOptions{FieldManager: \\\"mycontroller\\\", Force: true})\\n37: \\n38: Note in this example that HorizontalPodAutoscaler is imported from an \\\"applyconfigurations\\\"\\n39: package. Each \\\"apply configuration\\\" type represents the same Kubernetes object kind as the\\n40: corresponding go struct, but where all fields are pointers to make them optional, allowing apply\\n41: requests to be accurately represented. For example, this when the apply configuration in the above\\n42: example is marshalled to YAML, it produces:\\n43: \\n44: \\tapiVersion: autoscaling/v1\\n45: \\tkind: HorizontalPodAutoscaler\\n46: \\tmetadata:\\n47: \\t    name: myHPA\\n48: \\t    namespace: myNamespace\\n49: \\tspec:\\n50: \\t    minReplicas: 0\\n51: \\n52: To understand why this is needed, the above YAML cannot be produced by the\\n53: v1.HorizontalPodAutoscaler go struct. Take for example:\\n54: \\n55: \\thpa := v1.HorizontalPodAutoscaler{\\n56: \\t     TypeMeta: metav1.TypeMeta{\\n57: \\t              APIVersion: \\\"autoscaling/v1\\\",\\n58: \\t              Kind:       \\\"HorizontalPodAutoscaler\\\",\\n59: \\t     },\\n60: \\t     ObjectMeta: ObjectMeta{\\n61: \\t              Namespace: ns,\\n62: \\t              Name:      autoscalerName,\\n63: \\t     },\\n64: \\t     Spec: v1.HorizontalPodAutoscalerSpec{\\n65: \\t              MinReplicas: pointer.Int32Ptr(0),\\n66: \\t     },\\n67: \\t}\\n68: \\n69: The above code attempts to declare the same apply configuration as shown in the previous examples,\\n70: but when marshalled to YAML, produces:\\n71: \\n72: \\tkind: HorizontalPodAutoscaler\\n73: \\tapiVersion: autoscaling/v1\\n74: \\tmetadata:\\n75: \\t  name: myHPA\\n76: \\t  namespace: myNamespace\\n77: \\tspec:\\n78: \\t  scaleTargetRef:\\n79: \\t    kind: \\\"\\\"\\n80: \\t    name: \\\"\\\"\\n81: \\t  minReplicas: 0\\n82: \\t  maxReplicas: 0\\n83: \\n84: Which, among other things, contains spec.maxReplicas set to 0. This is almost certainly not what\\n85: the caller intended (the intended apply configuration says nothing about the maxReplicas field),\\n86: and could have serious consequences on a production system: it directs the autoscaler to downscale\\n87: to zero pods. The problem here originates from the fact that the go structs contain required fields\\n88: that are zero valued if not set explicitly. The go structs work as intended for create and update\\n89: operations, but are fundamentally incompatible with apply, which is why we have introduced the\\n90: generated \\\"apply configuration\\\" types.\\n91: \\n92: The \\\"apply configurations\\\" also have convenience With<FieldName> functions that make it easier to\\n93: build apply requests. This allows developers to set fields without having to deal with the fact that\\n94: all the fields in the \\\"apply configuration\\\" types are pointers, and are inconvenient to set using\\n95: go. For example \\\"MinReplicas: &0\\\" is not legal go code, so without the With functions, developers\\n96: would work around this problem by using a library, .e.g. \\\"MinReplicas: pointer.Int32Ptr(0)\\\", but\\n97: string enumerations like corev1.Protocol are still a problem since they cannot be supported by a\\n98: general purpose library. In addition to the convenience, the With functions also isolate\\n99: developers from the underlying representation, which makes it safer for the underlying\\n100: representation to be changed to support additional features in the future.\\n101: \\n102: # Controller Support\\n103: \\n104: The new client-go support makes it much easier to use Server-side Apply in controllers, by either of\\n105: two mechanisms.\\n106: \\n107: Mechanism 1:\\n108: \\n109: When authoring new controllers to use Server-side Apply, a good approach is to have the controller\\n110: recreate the apply configuration for an object each time it reconciles that object.  This ensures\\n111: that the controller fully reconciles all the fields that it is responsible for. Controllers\\n112: typically should unconditionally set all the fields they own by setting \\\"Force: true\\\" in the\\n113: ApplyOptions. Controllers must also provide a FieldManager name that is unique to the\\n114: reconciliation loop that apply is called from.\\n115: \\n116: When upgrading existing controllers to use Server-side Apply the same approach often works\\n117: well--migrate the controllers to recreate the apply configuration each time it reconciles any\\n118: object. For cases where this does not work well, see Mechanism 2.\\n119: \\n120: Mechanism 2:\\n121: \\n122: When upgrading existing controllers to use Server-side Apply, the controller might have multiple\\n123: code paths that update different parts of an object depending on various conditions. Migrating a\\n124: controller like this to Server-side Apply can be risky because if the controller forgets to include\\n125: any fields in an apply configuration that is included in a previous apply request, a field can be\\n126: accidentally deleted. For such cases, an alternative to mechanism 1 is to replace any controller\\n127: reconciliation code that performs a \\\"read/modify-in-place/update\\\" (or patch) workflow with a\\n128: \\\"extract/modify-in-place/apply\\\" workflow. Here's an example of the new workflow:\\n129: \\n130: \\t    fieldMgr := \\\"my-field-manager\\\"\\n131: \\t    deploymentClient := clientset.AppsV1().Deployments(\\\"default\\\")\\n132: \\t    // read, could also be read from a shared informer\\n133: \\t    deployment, err := deploymentClient.Get(ctx, \\\"example-deployment\\\", metav1.GetOptions{})\\n134: \\t    if err != nil {\\n135: \\t      // handle error\\n136: \\t    }\\n137: \\t    // extract\\n138: \\t    deploymentApplyConfig, err := appsv1ac.ExtractDeployment(deployment, fieldMgr)\\n139: \\t    if err != nil {\\n140: \\t      // handle error\\n141: \\t    }\\n142: \\t    // modify-in-place\\n143: \\t    deploymentApplyConfig.Spec.Template.Spec.WithContainers(corev1ac.Container().\\n144: \\t\\tWithName(\\\"modify-slice\\\").\\n145: \\t\\tWithImage(\\\"nginx:1.14.2\\\"),\\n146: \\t    )\\n147: \\t    // apply\\n148: \\t    applied, err := deploymentClient.Apply(ctx, extractedDeployment, metav1.ApplyOptions{FieldManager: fieldMgr})\\n149: */\\n150: package applyconfigurations\\n151: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/applyconfigurations/doc.go\"}",

    "content": "1: /*\n2: Copyright 2021 The Kubernetes Authors.\n3: \n4: Licensed under the Apache License, Version 2.0 (the \"License\");\n5: you may not use this file except in compliance with the License.\n6: You may obtain a copy of the License at\n7: \n8:     http://www.apache.org/licenses/LICENSE-2.0\n9: \n10: Unless required by applicable law or agreed to in writing, software\n11: distributed under the License is distributed on an \"AS IS\" BASIS,\n12: WITHOUT WARRANTIES OR CONDITIONS OF ANY KIND, either express or implied.\n13: See the License for the specific language governing permissions and\n14: limitations under the License.\n15: */\n16: \n17: /*\n18: Package applyconfigurations provides typesafe go representations of the apply\n19: configurations that are used to constructs Server-side Apply requests.\n20: \n21: # Basics\n22: \n23: The Apply functions in the typed client (see the k8s.io/client-go/kubernetes/typed packages) offer\n24: a direct and typesafe way of calling Server-side Apply. Each Apply function takes an \"apply\n25: configuration\" type as an argument, which is a structured representation of an Apply request. For\n26: example:\n27: \n28: \timport (\n29: \t     ...\n30: \t     v1ac \"k8s.io/client-go/applyconfigurations/autoscaling/v1\"\n31: \t)\n32: \thpaApplyConfig := v1ac.HorizontalPodAutoscaler(autoscalerName, ns).\n33: \t     WithSpec(v1ac.HorizontalPodAutoscalerSpec().\n34: \t              WithMinReplicas(0)\n35: \t     )\n36: \treturn hpav1client.Apply(ctx, hpaApplyConfig, metav1.ApplyOptions{FieldManager: \"mycontroller\", Force: true})\n37: \n38: Note in this example that HorizontalPodAutoscaler is imported from an \"applyconfigurations\"\n39: package. Each \"apply configuration\" type represents the same Kubernetes object kind as the\n40: corresponding go struct, but where all fields are pointers to make them optional, allowing apply\n41: requests to be accurately represented. For example, this when the apply configuration in the above\n42: example is marshalled to YAML, it produces:\n43: \n44: \tapiVersion: autoscaling/v1\n45: \tkind: HorizontalPodAutoscaler\n46: \tmetadata:\n47: \t    name: myHPA\n48: \t    namespace: myNamespace\n49: \tspec:\n50: \t    minReplicas: 0\n51: \n52: To understand why this is needed, the above YAML cannot be produced by the\n53: v1.HorizontalPodAutoscaler go struct. Take for example:\n54: \n55: \thpa := v1.HorizontalPodAutoscaler{\n56: \t     TypeMeta: metav1.TypeMeta{\n57: \t              APIVersion: \"autoscaling/v1\",\n58: \t              Kind:       \"HorizontalPodAutoscaler\",\n59: \t     },\n60: \t     ObjectMeta: ObjectMeta{\n61: \t              Namespace: ns,\n62: \t              Name:      autoscalerName,\n63: \t     },\n64: \t     Spec: v1.HorizontalPodAutoscalerSpec{\n65: \t              MinReplicas: pointer.Int32Ptr(0),\n66: \t     },\n67: \t}\n68: \n69: The above code attempts to declare the same apply configuration as shown in the previous examples,\n70: but when marshalled to YAML, produces:\n71: \n72: \tkind: HorizontalPodAutoscaler\n73: \tapiVersion: autoscaling/v1\n74: \tmetadata:\n75: \t  name: myHPA\n76: \t  namespace: myNamespace\n77: \tspec:\n78: \t  scaleTargetRef:\n79: \t    kind: \"\"\n80: \t    name: \"\"\n81: \t  minReplicas: 0\n82: \t  maxReplicas: 0\n83: \n84: Which, among other things, contains spec.maxReplicas set to 0. This is almost certainly not what\n85: the caller intended (the intended apply configuration says nothing about the maxReplicas field),\n86: and could have serious consequences on a production system: it directs the autoscaler to downscale\n87: to zero pods. The problem here originates from the fact that the go structs contain required fields\n88: that are zero valued if not set explicitly. The go structs work as intended for create and update\n89: operations, but are fundamentally incompatible with apply, which is why we have introduced the\n90: generated \"apply configuration\" types.\n91: \n92: The \"apply configurations\" also have convenience With<FieldName> functions that make it easier to\n93: build apply requests. This allows developers to set fields without having to deal with the fact that\n94: all the fields in the \"apply configuration\" types are pointers, and are inconvenient to set using\n95: go. For example \"MinReplicas: &0\" is not legal go code, so without the With functions, developers\n96: would work around this problem by using a library, .e.g. \"MinReplicas: pointer.Int32Ptr(0)\", but\n97: string enumerations like corev1.Protocol are still a problem since they cannot be supported by a\n98: general purpose library. In addition to the convenience, the With functions also isolate\n99: developers from the underlying representation, which makes it safer for the underlying\n100: representation to be changed to support additional features in the future.\n101: \n102: # Controller Support\n103: \n104: The new client-go support makes it much easier to use Server-side Apply in controllers, by either of\n105: two mechanisms.\n106: \n107: Mechanism 1:\n108: \n109: When authoring new controllers to use Server-side Apply, a good approach is to have the controller\n110: recreate the apply configuration for an object each time it reconciles that object.  This ensures\n111: that the controller fully reconciles all the fields that it is responsible for. Controllers\n112: typically should unconditionally set all the fields they own by setting \"Force: true\" in the\n113: ApplyOptions. Controllers must also provide a FieldManager name that is unique to the\n114: reconciliation loop that apply is called from.\n115: \n116: When upgrading existing controllers to use Server-side Apply the same approach often works\n117: well--migrate the controllers to recreate the apply configuration each time it reconciles any\n118: object. For cases where this does not work well, see Mechanism 2.\n119: \n120: Mechanism 2:\n121: \n122: When upgrading existing controllers to use Server-side Apply, the controller might have multiple\n123: code paths that update different parts of an object depending on various conditions. Migrating a\n124: controller like this to Server-side Apply can be risky because if the controller forgets to include\n125: any fields in an apply configuration that is included in a previous apply request, a field can be\n126: accidentally deleted. For such cases, an alternative to mechanism 1 is to replace any controller\n127: reconciliation code that performs a \"read/modify-in-place/update\" (or patch) workflow with a\n128: \"extract/modify-in-place/apply\" workflow. Here's an example of the new workflow:\n129: \n130: \t    fieldMgr := \"my-field-manager\"\n131: \t    deploymentClient := clientset.AppsV1().Deployments(\"default\")\n132: \t    // read, could also be read from a shared informer\n133: \t    deployment, err := deploymentClient.Get(ctx, \"example-deployment\", metav1.GetOptions{})\n134: \t    if err != nil {\n135: \t      // handle error\n136: \t    }\n137: \t    // extract\n138: \t    deploymentApplyConfig, err := appsv1ac.ExtractDeployment(deployment, fieldMgr)\n139: \t    if err != nil {\n140: \t      // handle error\n141: \t    }\n142: \t    // modify-in-place\n143: \t    deploymentApplyConfig.Spec.Template.Spec.WithContainers(corev1ac.Container().\n144: \t\tWithName(\"modify-slice\").\n145: \t\tWithImage(\"nginx:1.14.2\"),\n146: \t    )\n147: \t    // apply\n148: \t    applied, err := deploymentClient.Apply(ctx, extractedDeployment, metav1.ApplyOptions{FieldManager: fieldMgr})\n149: */\n150: package applyconfigurations\n151: ",

  "content": "{\"content\":\"1: # `client-go` Architecture\\n2: \\n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\\n5: \\n6: ## Client Configuration\\n7: \\n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\\n13: \\n14: ## REST Client\\n15: \\n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\\n19: \\n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\\n23: is injected into requests.\\n24: \\n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\\n28: \\n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\\n30: \\n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \\n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\\n46: \\n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\\n48: \\n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\\n57: \\n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\\n59: \\n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\\n61: models.\\n62: \\n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\\n64: \\n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\\n71: \\n72: ## Code Generation\\n73: \\n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\\n77: several key components:\\n78: \\n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\\n83: \\n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\\n86: \\n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\\n88: \\n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\\n90: \\n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\\n93: \\n94: The data flow is as follows:\\n95: \\n96: ```mermaid\\n97: graph TD\\n98:     subgraph \\\"Kubernetes API\\\"\\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\\n100:     end\\n101: \\n102:     subgraph \\\"client-go: Informer Mechanism\\\"\\n103:         Reflector(\\\"1. Reflector\\\")\\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\\\"2. DeltaFIFO\\\")\\n105:         Indexer[\\\"3. Indexer (Cache)\\\"]\\n106:         EventHandlers(\\\"4. Event Handlers\\\")\\n107:     end\\n108: \\n109:     subgraph \\\"User Code\\\"\\n110:         WorkQueue[\\\"5. Work Queue\\\"]\\n111:         Controller(\\\"6. Controller\\\")\\n112:     end\\n113: \\n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\\n121: ```\\n122: \\n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\\n126: **\\\"too old\\\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\\n129: \\n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\\n132: \\n133: ## Controller Patterns\\n134: \\n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\\n136: ensure resiliency.\\n137: \\n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\\n142: \\n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\\n145: shared `Lease` object.\\n146: \\n147: ## Server-Side Apply\\n148: \\n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\\n153: construct the declarative patch.\\n154: \\n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\\n156: \\n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\\n159: \\n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\\n163: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md\"}",

To make this recovery more efficient, the\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\n129: \n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\n132: \n133: ## Controller Patterns\n134: \n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\n136: ensure resiliency.\n137: \n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\n142: \n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\n145: shared `Lease` object.\n146: \n147: ## Server-Side Apply\n148: \n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\n153: construct the declarative patch.\n154: \n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\n156: \n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\n159: \n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\n163: ",
    "url": "https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md"
  }
}

{
  "content": "# Kubernetes Server-Side Apply and applyconfigurations: A Deep Guide\n\n---\n\n## 1. Subsystem Purpose, Boundaries, and Dependencies\n\n### What is Server-Side Apply?\n\nServer-Side Apply (SSA) is a declarative object management mechanism introduced in Kubernetes 1.18 (GA in 1.22). Unlike the traditional `GET \u2192 modify \u2192 PUT` (or strategic-merge-patch) workflow, SSA moves the merge logic to the server. Each actor\u2014called a **field manager**\u2014declares the fields it intends to own and their desired values. The server maintains a `metadata.managedFields` table that records which manager owns each field. When multiple actors apply to the same object, the server ensures they cannot silently overwrite each other's fields; attempting to set a field already owned by another manager raises an explicit conflict error, unless the request carries `force: true`.\n\n### Where This Lives\n\nThe SSA subsystem spans three packages:\n\n| Package | Role |\n|---|---|\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/applyconfigurations/` | Client-side: generated, type-safe patch builders |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/util/managedfields/` | Shared: field management middleware (used by both client and server) |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/endpoints/handlers/patch.go` | Server-side: HTTP PATCH handler that routes apply requests |\n\nThese three packages form a layered stack, but they have a hard dependency on the external library `sigs.k8s.io/structured-merge-diff` (SMD, v6), which provides the schema-aware merge algorithm, conflict detection, and fieldpath data structures. **The Kubernetes codebase does not contain the SMD merge algorithm itself**\u2014it only provides the adapters and middleware around it.\n\n### Upstream and Downstream Dependencies\n\n- **Downstream consumers**: Any controller or operator that calls `.Apply(...)` on a typed client from `k8s.io/client-go/kubernetes/typed/...` uses this subsystem. So does the `kubectl apply --server-side` command.\n- **Upstream dependencies**: The `managedfields` package requires OpenAPI v3 schema for type-aware merging of built-in resources, and the `k8s.io/kube-openapi/pkg/util/proto` package to parse that schema into the SMD model.\n- **CRD variant**: For Custom Resource Definitions, a separate code path uses a \"deduced\" type converter that treats all map values atomically (no schema-driven list merging).\n\n---\n\n## 2. Key Components and Responsibility Split\n\n### 2.1 Generated Apply Configuration Types (`client-go/applyconfigurations/`)\n\nThe `applyconfigurations` package contains code generated by `applyconfiguration-gen`. For every Kubernetes API type (e.g., `apps/v1.Deployment`), there is a parallel mirror struct (e.g., `DeploymentApplyConfiguration`) where **every field is a pointer**. This is the foundational design decision: absent fields produce `omitempty` in JSON/YAML, ensuring only explicitly-stated fields appear in the patch.\n\n```\n// From staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/applyconfigurations/meta/v1/objectmeta.go\ntype ObjectMetaApplyConfiguration struct {\n    Name      *string `json:\"name,omitempty\"`\n    Namespace *string `json:\"namespace,omitempty\"`\n    Labels    map[string]string `json:\"labels,omitempty\"`\n    ...\n}\n```\n\nEach field has a `With<FieldName>(value T)` builder method returning a pointer to the receiver, enabling chained calls. This both avoids the awkward `&value` idiom required to set pointer fields and insulates callers from the internal representation.\n\nThe `utils.go` file (generated, 1920 lines) exposes a top-level `ForKind(schema.GroupVersionKind)` function that maps a GVK to its empty apply configuration, enabling dynamic dispatch in generic code.\n\nThe `meta/v1/unstructured.go` file provides `UnstructuredExtractor`, which is discussed in section 4.\n\n### 2.2 The `managedfields` Package and the Manager Stack\n\n`staging/src/k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/util/managedfields/` is the workhorse. The public surface is a single `FieldManager` type (aliased from the internal package) with two methods:\n\n- `Update(liveObj, newObj, manager)` \u2014 for non-apply mutations (PUT, PATCH with other strategies). Records which fields the manager touched.\n- `Apply(liveObj, appliedObj, manager, force)` \u2014 for SSA. Merges the applied configuration onto the live object, detects conflicts, and returns the new merged object plus updated `managedFields`.\n\nInternally, `FieldManager` delegates to a **chain of decorator managers** assembled in `NewDefaultFieldManager`:\n\n```\n// From staging/src/k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/util/managedfields/internal/fieldmanager.go\nNewVersionCheckManager(\n  NewLastAppliedUpdater(\n    NewLastAppliedManager(\n      NewProbabilisticSkipNonAppliedManager(\n        NewCapManagersManager(\n          NewBuildManagerInfoManager(\n            NewManagedFieldsUpdater(\n              NewStripMetaManager(f),  // f = structuredMergeManager\n            ), kind.GroupVersion(), subresource,\n          ), DefaultMaxUpdateManagers,  // = 10\n        ), objectCreater, DefaultTrackOnCreateProbability,  // = 1.0\n      ), typeConverter, objectConverter, kind.GroupVersion(),\n    ),\n  ), kind,\n), subresource,\n```\n\nEach decorator in the chain implements the `Manager` interface (`internal/manager.go`):\n\n```go\ntype Manager interface {\n    Update(liveObj, newObj runtime.Object, managed Managed, manager string) (runtime.Object, Managed, error)\n    Apply(liveObj, appliedObj runtime.Object, managed Managed, fieldManager string, force bool) (runtime.Object, Managed, error)\n}\n```\n\nThe innermost manager, `structuredMergeManager`, calls into the SMD library directly.\n\n### 2.3 The HTTP PATCH Handler (`apiserver/pkg/endpoints/handlers/patch.go`)\n\n`PatchResource` is the HTTP handler factory registered for `PATCH` requests. It reads the `Content-Type` header to determine the patch strategy:\n\n- `application/json-patch+json` \u2192 `jsonPatcher`\n- `application/merge-patch+json` \u2192 `jsonPatcher` (strategic merge path)\n- `application/strategic-merge-patch+json` \u2192 `smpPatcher`\n- `application/apply-patch+yaml` \u2192 `applyPatcher` \u2190 SSA\n- `application/apply-patch+cbor` \u2192 `applyPatcher` (CBOR variant, gated by feature flag)\n\nOnly the `applyPatcher` invokes `FieldManager.Apply`. The others call `FieldManager.UpdateNoErrors`, which tracks field ownership for non-apply writes but never raises conflict errors.\n\n---\n\n## 3. End-to-End Request Flow\n\n### 3.1 Client Side\n\nA controller building an SSA request:\n\n```go\n// staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/example_test.go:303\ndeploymentApplyConfig := appsv1ac.Deployment(deploymentName, \"default\").\n    WithSpec(appsv1ac.DeploymentSpec().\n        WithReplicas(replicas).\n        WithSelector(metav1ac.LabelSelector().WithMatchLabels(map[string]string{\"app\": \"my-app\"})).\n        WithTemplate(corev1ac.PodTemplateSpec()...),\n    )\n\nresult, err := clientset.AppsV1().Deployments(\"default\").Apply(\n    context.TODO(),\n    deploymentApplyConfig,\n    metav1.ApplyOptions{FieldManager: \"my-controller\"},\n)\n```\n\nInternally the typed client serializes `deploymentApplyConfig` to YAML (only non-nil pointer fields appear) and issues a `PATCH` request with `Content-Type: application/apply-patch+yaml` and the query param `fieldManager=my-controller`.\n\n### 3.2 Server-Side: Handler Routing\n\n`PatchResource` (in `patch.go:68`) dispatches to `applyPatcher.applyPatchToCurrentObject`. This calls:\n\n```go\n// patch.go:514\nobj, err := p.fieldManager.Apply(obj, patchObj, p.options.FieldManager, force)\n```\n\nThe `fieldManager` here is `scope.FieldManager`, an instance of `*managedfields.FieldManager` that was pre-built during API server startup via `NewDefaultFieldManager` or `NewDefaultCRDFieldManager`.\n\nFor SSA, `p.forceAllowCreate = true`, meaning if the object does not yet exist in etcd, the apply creates it (via `GuaranteedUpdate`'s create path). The actual store write goes through `p.restPatcher.Update(...)`, which calls `GuaranteedUpdate` on the underlying storage.\n\nThe transform chain is: `applyPatch \u2192 applyAdmission \u2192 dedupOwnerReferences`. Admission runs after the SSA merge, so admission webhooks see the fully-merged object, not just the patch.\n\n### 3.3 Inside `FieldManager.Apply`\n\n```go\n// internal/fieldmanager.go:183\nfunc (f *FieldManager) Apply(liveObj, appliedObj runtime.Object, manager string, force bool) (object runtime.Object, err error) {\n    managed, err := DecodeManagedFields(accessor.GetManagedFields())\n    object, managed, err = f.fieldManager.Apply(liveObj, appliedObj, managed, manager, force)\n    if conflicts, ok := err.(merge.Conflicts); ok {\n        return nil, NewConflictError(conflicts)\n    }\n    EncodeObjectManagedFields(object, managed)\n    return object, nil\n}\n```\n\nThe `managed` value is decoded from the live object's `metadata.managedFields` (a JSON-encoded field-set per manager). It is passed through every decorator layer. At the end, `EncodeObjectManagedFields` writes the updated `managedFields` back onto the returned object before it is persisted.\n\n### 3.4 Decorator Execution Order for `Apply`\n\nWhen `Apply` is called, execution flows from the outermost to innermost decorator:\n\n1. **`versionCheckManager`** (`versioncheck.go:47`): Rejects if `appliedObj.GVK != expected GVK`. Prevents accidental cross-version applies.\n\n2. **`lastAppliedUpdater`** (`lastappliedupdater.go`): After the inner chain succeeds, conditionally sets the `kubectl.kubernetes.io/last-applied-configuration` annotation on the live object (only for kubectl applies).\n\n3. **`lastAppliedManager`** (`lastappliedmanager.go:59`): Enables kubectl client-side apply \u2192 server-side apply migration. If the manager is `\"kubectl\"` and there are conflicts, it checks whether each conflicting field is covered by the `kubectl.kubernetes.io/last-applied-configuration` annotation on the live object. Fields in the last-applied annotation are treated as previously \"owned\" by kubectl, so the conflict is silently force-resolved. This allows a seamless upgrade from `kubectl apply` (client-side) to `kubectl apply --server-side` without manual ownership cleanup.\n\n4. **`skipNonAppliedManager`** (`skipnonapplied.go:76`): On the first SSA apply to an object that has never been applied to (empty `managedFields`), it first calls `Update` with the live object against an empty object to create a `\"before-first-apply\"` manager entry for all existing fields. This prevents the first SSA caller from accidentally becoming the owner of all pre-existing fields.\n\n5. **`capManagersManager`** (`capmanagers.go`): After an `Update`, if the number of update-type manager entries exceeds `DefaultMaxUpdateManagers` (10), the oldest entries are merged into a versioned bucket named `\"ancient-changes\"`. This prevents unbounded growth of `managedFields` due to many distinct update managers.\n\n6. **`buildManagerInfoManager`** (`buildmanagerinfo.go`): Stamps each manager entry with a timestamp and API version, building the JSON-encoded manager identifier used as the key in `managedFields`.\n\n7. **`managedFieldsUpdater`** (`managedfieldsupdater.go`): Re-encodes the `Managed` state back into `metadata.managedFields` format on the returned object.\n\n8. **`stripMetaManager`** (`stripmeta.go:36`): Removes a hardcoded set of paths from the manager's tracked field set:\n   - `apiVersion`, `kind`\n   - `metadata`, `metadata.name`, `metadata.namespace`, `metadata.creationTimestamp`, `metadata.selfLink`, `metadata.uid`, `metadata.clusterName`, `metadata.generation`, `metadata.managedFields`, `metadata.resourceVersion`\n   \n   These fields are infrastructure fields; tracking them as owned would cause spurious conflicts.\n\n9. **`structuredMergeManager`** (`structuredmerge.go:121`): The terminal manager. Converts the live object and patch object to SMD typed values via `TypeConverter.ObjectToTyped`, then calls `f.updater.Apply(liveObjTyped, patchObjTyped, apiVersion, managed.Fields(), manager, force)`. The SMD library computes the merged result and detects conflicts. The merged typed value is converted back to a `runtime.Object` using `TypeConverter.TypedToObject`.\n\n---\n\n## 4. Extract Workflow (Mechanism 2 for Controllers)\n\nWhen a controller cannot safely reconstruct the full desired state on every reconcile (e.g., it has many conditionally-set code paths), it uses the extract-then-apply pattern described in the package doc:\n\n```go\n// From staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/applyconfigurations/doc.go:138\ndeploymentApplyConfig, err := appsv1ac.ExtractDeployment(deployment, fieldMgr)\n// modify only what changed\ndeploymentApplyConfig.Spec.Template.Spec.WithContainers(...)\napplied, err := deploymentClient.Apply(ctx, deploymentApplyConfig, metav1.ApplyOptions{FieldManager: fieldMgr})\n```\n\n`ExtractDeployment` delegates to `managedfields.ExtractInto`:\n\n```go\n// staging/src/k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/util/managedfields/extract.go:53\nfunc ExtractInto(object runtime.Object, objectType typed.ParseableType, fieldManager string, applyConfiguration interface{}, subresource string) error {\n    typedObj, _ := toTyped(object, objectType)\n    accessor, _ := meta.Accessor(object)\n    fieldsEntry, ok := findManagedFields(accessor, fieldManager, subresource)\n    // ...\n    fieldset := &fieldpath.Set{}\n    fieldset.FromJSON(bytes.NewReader(fieldsEntry.FieldsV1.Raw))\n    u := typedObj.ExtractItems(fieldset.Leaves()).AsValue().Unstructured()\n    runtime.DefaultUnstructuredConverter.FromUnstructured(u.(map[string]interface{}), applyConfiguration)\n}\n```\n\nThe function:\n1. Finds the `ManagedFieldsEntry` where `Manager == fieldManager` and `Operation == Apply`.\n2. Deserializes the field-set from JSON (the `FieldsV1` blob).\n3. Calls `typedObj.ExtractItems(fieldset.Leaves())` to reconstruct only those field paths from the live object.\n4. Converts the result to the typed apply configuration struct.\n\n**For unstructured objects** (dynamic client users), the `UnstructuredExtractor` in `meta/v1/unstructured.go` performs the same logic but returns an `*unstructured.Unstructured`. It fetches the OpenAPI schema via the discovery client, building a `GvkParser` (from `kube-openapi`) that provides a `typed.ParseableType` for the object's GVK. The parser is cached behind a `gvkParserCache` with a 1-minute TTL (`openAPISchemaTTL`):\n\n```go\n// staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/applyconfigurations/meta/v1/unstructured.go:33\nconst openAPISchemaTTL = time.Minute\n```\n\n---\n\n## 5. Conflict Handling\n\n### 5.1 Detection\n\nConflict detection occurs inside the SMD library's `merge.Updater.Apply` call. The library computes the field-set of the incoming patch object, intersects it with the `managedFields` of all other managers, and reports any intersection as a `merge.Conflicts` value (a slice of `merge.Conflict` structs, each containing a manager name and a field path).\n\n### 5.2 Error Representation\n\n`NewConflictError` in `internal/conflict.go` converts `merge.Conflicts` into a `*errors.StatusError` with HTTP status 409 and a `Reason` of `\"Conflict\"`. Each conflicting field becomes a `metav1.StatusCause` of type `CauseTypeFieldManagerConflict`, with the `Field` holding the SMD path string and the `Message` identifying the conflicting manager:\n\n```\nApply failed with 2 conflicts:\nconflicts with \"other-controller\":\n- .spec.replicas\nconflicts with \"kube-controller-manager\" using apps/v1 at 2024-01-01T00:00:00Z:\n- .spec.template.spec.containers[name=\"nginx\"].image\n```\n\nThe manager is printed in human-readable form: the JSON-encoded manager key is decoded back into a `ManagedFieldsEntry` struct to extract `Manager`, `Subresource`, `Operation`, `APIVersion`, and `Time`.\n\n### 5.3 Force Resolution\n\nSetting `force: true` in `ApplyOptions` instructs the SMD library to steal ownership of conflicting fields from their current owners. The field manager of the old owner loses those paths in its entry. This is appropriate for controllers that unconditionally own the full desired state of their managed resources.\n\n### 5.4 kubectl Migration Path\n\nThe `lastAppliedManager` layer (`lastappliedmanager.go`) provides a controlled conflict bypass for kubectl. When upgrading from `kubectl apply` (client-side) to `kubectl apply --server-side`, the first SSA apply will conflict with the pre-existing `last-applied-configuration` annotation because no managed fields were previously tracked. The manager reads the annotation, computes what fields it declared, and allows the conflicts for fields that match\u2014without requiring the user to pass `force: true`. Fields not in the annotation still raise errors.\n\n---\n\n## 6. Subresource Handling: `ScaleHandler`\n\nThe scale subresource (`/scale`) exposes a different API type (`autoscaling/v1.Scale`) that maps onto fields inside the parent resource (e.g., `spec.replicas` in Deployment). Because the field paths differ between the parent and the subresource view, `ScaleHandler` in `managedfields/scalehandler.go` provides bidirectional translation:\n\n- **`ToSubresource`**: Filters the parent's `managedFields`, keeping only entries that include the resource-specific replicas path, and remaps that path to `spec.replicas` in `autoscaling/v1` terms.\n- **`ToParent`**: Merges scale manager entries back into the parent's `managedFields`, translating the scale path back to the parent's `spec.replicas` path.\n\nThis ensures that a controller using `/scale` to change replica counts is correctly attributed ownership of `spec.replicas` in the parent object's `managedFields`.\n\n---\n\n## 7. Failure Modes, Limits, and Design Tradeoffs\n\n### 7.1 Object Too Large\n\nThe apply patcher does not have a managedFields-stripping fallback for SSA applies. For non-apply patches (JSON, strategic merge), if the object exceeds etcd's size limit after serialization, the server retries after stripping `managedFields` from the object:\n\n```go\n// patch.go:713\nif isTooLargeError(err) && p.patchType != types.ApplyYAMLPatchType && p.patchType != types.ApplyCBORPatchType {\n    // retry without managedFields for non-apply patches\n}\n```\n\nFor SSA applies, no such retry exists. A conflict between object size and the cost of tracking managed fields must be resolved by the operator (e.g., reducing the number of field managers).\n\n### 7.2 Manager Entry Cap\n\n`DefaultMaxUpdateManagers = 10` (`internal/fieldmanager.go:35`). When the number of distinct update managers exceeds this, `capManagersManager` merges the oldest into per-version `\"ancient-changes\"` buckets. This loses per-manager granularity. The comment in the code acknowledges this is a heuristic and the correct value is uncertain.\n\n### 7.3 CRD vs. Built-in Resources\n\n`NewDefaultCRDFieldManager` uses `NewCRDStructuredMergeManager`, which injects `newCRDVersionConverter` instead of the standard `newVersionConverter`. CRDs may not have a schema, so the type converter falls back to `NewDeducedTypeConverter()`, which treats all maps atomically. This means for schemaless CRDs, list merging does not use associative key semantics\u2014the whole list is treated as atomic.\n\n### 7.4 `managedFields` Must Be Nil in Apply Requests\n\nThe `structuredMergeManager.Apply` method explicitly rejects apply requests that include `metadata.managedFields`:\n\n```go\n// structuredmerge.go:135\nif patchObjMeta.GetManagedFields() != nil {\n    return nil, nil, errors.NewBadRequest(\"metadata.managedFields must be nil\")\n}\n```\n\nClients must not include `managedFields` in their patch bodies. The `applyconfigurations` types do not include a `ManagedFields` field, enforcing this at compile time.\n\n### 7.5 Version Mismatch\n\nThe patch object's GVK is validated twice: once by `versionCheckManager` (GVK equality) and once by `structuredMergeManager` (group-version equality against the handler's registered version). Submitting a patch for `apps/v1beta2.Deployment` to an `apps/v1.Deployment` endpoint returns a 400 Bad Request.\n\n### 7.6 `skipNonAppliedManager` and Bootstrap Probability\n\n`DefaultTrackOnCreateProbability = 1` means field tracking is started on 100% of object creations. This can be tuned down if the overhead of tracking `managedFields` at creation time is a concern. Setting it to 0 defers tracking until the first SSA apply, using `\"before-first-apply\"` as the synthetic manager for all pre-existing fields.\n\n---\n\n## 8. Extension Points and Customization Hooks\n\n### 8.1 `TypeConverter`\n\nThe `TypeConverter` interface (`managedfields/typeconverter.go`) is the primary extension point:\n\n```go\ntype TypeConverter = internal.TypeConverter\n```\n\nThree implementations are provided:\n- `NewTypeConverter(openapiSpec, preserveUnknownFields)` \u2014 standard for built-in types.\n- `NewDeducedTypeConverter()` \u2014 for CRDs without a schema.\n- `NewSchemeTypeConverter(scheme, parser)` \u2014 using a runtime `Scheme` and typed parser for test environments.\n\nA third-party API server can supply a custom `TypeConverter` to integrate SSA with non-OpenAPI schemas.\n\n### 8.2 `resetFields` Filter\n\n`NewDefaultFieldManager` accepts a `resetFields map[fieldpath.APIVersion]fieldpath.Filter` argument. This is passed to the SMD `merge.Updater` as `IgnoreFilter`. Fields in this filter are not tracked in `managedFields` at all, regardless of who sets them. This is used for fields that should always be reset by the server (e.g., certain status fields that must not be owned by any client).\n\n### 8.3 Subresource Isolation\n\nThe `FieldManager` is parameterized by `subresource string`. Updates and applies to a subresource endpoint create `ManagedFieldsEntry` items with `Subresource` set, keeping them separate from the main resource's entries. The `findManagedFields` function in `extract.go` matches on both `Manager` and `Subresource`, so the extract workflow correctly handles subresource contexts.\n\n### 8.4 Code Generation\n\nAll apply configuration types are generated by `applyconfiguration-gen` in `k8s.io/code-generator`. Developers adding a new built-in API type must run `hack/update-codegen.sh` to regenerate:\n- The per-type `*ApplyConfiguration` struct.\n- The `With<FieldName>` builder methods.\n- The `Extract<Type>` and `Extract<Type>Status` functions.\n- The `utils.go` dispatch table.\n\nOmitting regeneration causes the typed apply path to silently fall back to untyped behavior or fail compilation.\n\n---\n\n## 9. Operational Implications\n\n### 9.1 Storage Overhead from `managedFields`\n\nEvery object persisted in etcd carries its `managedFields` as part of the stored JSON. In clusters with many controllers each doing SSA applies, this can be significant. A Deployment managed by three controllers (e.g., a Helm controller, a network-policy injector, and a metrics-label stamper) will have three `ManagedFieldsEntry` values in `managedFields`, each containing a serialized fieldpath set. For objects with many fields, these sets can be kilobytes each.\n\nThe cap of 10 update managers (`DefaultMaxUpdateManagers`) helps bound storage growth from non-SSA actors, but apply managers are not capped\u2014each distinct `FieldManager` name used in an SSA apply gets its own entry and there is no cap on the number of apply entries.\n\n**Mitigation**: Operators should name their field managers consistently (one name per reconciliation loop), avoid unnecessary field changes, and prefer `Force: true` in controllers that fully own their fields to prevent orphaned ownership entries.\n\n### 9.2 OpenAPI Schema Caching for Extraction\n\nThe `UnstructuredExtractor` caches the parsed OpenAPI schema for 1 minute (`openAPISchemaTTL`). During this window, a newly-added CRD field will not be extractable. The cache is per-extractor instance and not shared across goroutines safely by default; the `gvkParserCache` uses a mutex, but callers must ensure they do not create a new extractor per request.\n\n### 9.3 Conflict Retries and Controller Overhead\n\nControllers using SSA with `Force: false` may experience conflict errors when another controller concurrently manages overlapping fields. This forces a retry loop. Each retry requires a GET (or informer read) and a PATCH. At scale, this can increase API server load. Best practice is to design controllers so field ownership is non-overlapping; `Force: true` is appropriate for controllers that are the sole authority over a well-defined set of fields.\n\n### 9.4 Apply as Create\n\nBecause `forceAllowCreate = true` is set for apply requests (`patch.go:674`), an SSA apply to a non-existent object creates it. The `GuaranteedUpdate` call hits the storage `Create` path. This can cause surprising behavior: an apply with `Force: false` against a non-existent resource will succeed (create), even though force semantics only matter for conflict resolution on existing objects.\n\n### 9.5 Debugging `managedFields`\n\nThe `managedFields` table is visible on every `kubectl get` with `-o json` or `-o yaml`. Each entry contains:\n- `manager`: the field manager name (decoded from the JSON key)\n- `operation`: `Apply` or `Update`\n- `apiVersion`: the version the manager last applied/updated in\n- `time`: timestamp of last operation\n- `fieldsV1`: a compact JSON representation of the owned field paths\n\nThe `fieldsV1` encoding uses a nested map structure where keys are field names prefixed with `f:` for fields and `k:` for associative list keys. The SMD library provides tooling to decode these for debugging.\n\n---\n\n## 10. File and Directory Map\n\n| Path | Description |\n|---|---|\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/applyconfigurations/` | Root of all generated apply configuration types |\n|
